Wright v. Grove Sun Newspaper Co. Inc.


No. 77,169


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1994 OK 37; 873 P.2d 983; 1994 Okla. LEXIS 45; 22 Media L. Rep. 1801



April 12, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ON CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIV. I



DISPOSITION: THE COURT OF APPEALS' OPINION IS VACATED AND THE TRIAL COURT'S DISMISSAL ORDER IS AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant accused filed tort actions against appellee newspaper. The Court of Appeals (Oklahoma) affirmed the district court's dismissal of the accused's tort claims against the newspaper, holding that the neutral reportage privilege provided the newspaper a complete defense to the accused's claims. The accused filed a petition for certiorari to the court of appeals.


OVERVIEW: The accused alleged two causes of action for libel and, as another theory of liability, the intentional infliction of emotional distress. The newspaper maintained that its claimed privilege rested upon the provisions of Okla. Stat. tit. XII, § 1443.1 (1991) or on the constitutional neutral reportage privilege. The court granted the accused's petition for certiorari. The court concluded that the district attorney's news conference was an official function of his office and represented a transaction or occasion to which the common-law fair report privilege would have attached. The references to the accused in the articles published by newspaper were accurate republications from materials released by the district attorney at the news conference and bereft of any judgmental newspaper gloss. Thus, under circumstances shown by the record, the fair report privilege afforded the newspaper a complete defense to the accused's libel claims. Because fair and accurate media coverage of official public occasions was in the highest and best interest of the public, the newspaper's conduct could not have been treated as actionable under the theory of intentional infliction of emotional distress.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the court of appeals and affirmed the district court's dismissal order.

In re Initiative Petition No. 358


No. 82,041


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1994 OK 27; 870 P.2d 782; 1994 Okla. LEXIS 30; 65 O.B.A.J. 886



February 24, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING PROTEST TO INITIATIVE PETITION No. 358, STATE QUESTION No. 658



DISPOSITION: INITIATIVE PETITION No. 358 IS SUFFICIENT TO BE SUBMITTED TO A VOTE OF THE PEOPLE AS STATE QUESTION No. 658.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Proponents, registered voters, filed a initiative petition with the Oklahoma Secretary of State for submission to the voters of the State of Oklahoma of a proposed Oklahoma Lottery Act, which would create a state lottery. Protestants, registered voters, challenged the initiative petition, contending that the proposed Act violated the Oklahoma Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The Act would create a lottery authority as an independent public body of the state that would be run with funds from the lottery. The protestants contended that the Act would unconstitutionally delegate fiscal policy making because the lottery authority would have unbridled authority to create the formula for determining gross revenues and net revenues, that it would create a private corporation with special privileges, and that it would amend existing law by reference. The court noted that it had jurisdiction to entertain protests to initiative petitions pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 34, § 8 (Supp. 1993), but that the doctrine of separation of powers prevented judicial interference with the initiative law-making process. Thus, the court's pre-election review of initiative petitions was limited under § 8 to clear or manifest facial constitutional infirmities. Because the protestants' contentions concerned the implementation of various provisions of the Act, their challenges were not reviewable through the court's inherent power to grant relief from costly expenditure of public revenues on needless election.


OUTCOME: The court held the initiative petition was sufficient for submission to a vote.

City of Del City v. FOP, Lodge No. 114


No. 73,138


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 169; 869 P.2d 309; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 190; 65 O.B.A.J. 44



December 21, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY. Honorable Bryan C. Dixon, District Judge



DISPOSITION: ORDER OF DISTRICT COURT REVERSED. CAUSE REMANDED FOR DISPOSITION CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the judgment of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which upheld the constitutionality of the "Evergreen" clause as it appeared in the Oklahoma Fire and Police Arbitration Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 11, § 51-105 (1991), and required that a collective bargaining agreement between the city and appellee unions remained in effect after its expiration.


OVERVIEW: The collective bargaining agreement between the parties expired before a new agreement had been reached. The city had filed a suit asking for a declaratory judgment as to the constitutionality of the Evergreen clause and alleged that a continuation of the collective bargaining agreement with respect to salaries and benefits as required by the Evergreen clause would have caused a deficit in the city's budget by extending it past the constitutionally permitted one year. The city also claimed that the parties had reached impasse. The unions urged the validity of the Evergreen clause asserting that the collective bargaining agreement did not establish a debt under Okla. Const. art. 10, § 26, regarding legislatively-mandated obligations. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and held that the perpetuation of a collective bargaining agreement beyond one year violated the art. 10, § 26, and that the Evergreen statute was unconstitutional, as it required municipalities to perpetuate contracts beyond the constitutionally-permitted one year.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case with instructions to enter judgment in favor of the city.

Houck v. Hold Oil Corp.


No. 69,492 Consolidated with No. 70,460


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 166; 867 P.2d 451; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 196; 65 O.B.A.J. 23; 128 Oil & Gas Rep. 93



December 21, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION I. APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF PITTSBURG COUNTY, STATE OF OKLAHOMA. Honorable Steven W. Taylor, Trial Judge



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS' OPINION. VACATED IN PART; JUDGMENT OF TRIAL COURT AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART; REMANDED FOR NEW TRIAL.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner oil corporation filed an application for a writ of certiorari, which sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oklahoma) affirming in part the damages awarded by the trial court to respondent landowners in respondents' action for injuries to the property caused by petitioner's drilling of wells on respondents' land.


OVERVIEW: Respondents purchased the surface estate to land subjected to an oil and gas lease subsequently assigned to the oil company. Respondents discovered that the company had destroyed trees on the land in the process of drilling two wells. One well was drilled prior to the effective date of the Surface Damage Act, Okla. Stat. Ann. tit. 52. § 318.2 et seq. (1982), the other after the effective date of the act. Respondents filed an action against the company for injuries to the property caused by the drilling of the wells. The trial court awarded respondents various damages and the appellate court affirmed. The court granted the company's petition for writ of certiorari and reversed the appellate court's decision, holding that the company should have been allowed to differentiate the injury caused by the first and second wells because the first well was drilled prior to the effective date of the act and was covered by the principles of common law. The second well was, however, covered by the act. Further, the record did not support an award of treble damages under the act because respondents failed to carry their burden to show that the company was a wilful violator of the act.


OUTCOME: The court granted the company's petition for writ of certiorari and reversed the appellate court's decision affirming the various damage awards to respondent in their action for injuries to their property caused by the company's drilling of wells.

 

78 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Hendrick v. Walters


No. 82,533


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 162; 865 P.2d 1232; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 193; 65 O.B.A.J. 33



December 16, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING FOR EXTRAORDINARY RELIEF



DISPOSITION: ORIGINAL JURISDICTION ASSUMED; THE COURT PRONOUNCES THE GOVERNOR HAS NOT FORFEITED HIS OFFICE BY FAILURE TO TAKE AN OATH IN THE FORM PRESCRIBED BY THE PROVISIONS OF 51 O.S.1961 § 2.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner senator instituted a proceeding alleging respondent governor forfeited his office by failing to take an oath in the form prescribed by the provisions of Okla. Stat. tit. 51, § 2 (1961). The governor contended that the passage of a legislative referendum No. 178, adopted in 1969 as Okla. Const. art. XV, § 1 effected a repeal of the statute by substitution.


OVERVIEW: Okla. Stat. tit. 51, § 2 (1961) required every state officer to take an oath to uphold the federal and state constitutions and discharge official duties without corruption. The senator instituted proceedings contending that the governor forfeited his office for failure to file a § 2 oath. The court assumed original cognizance of the issue and stated that it constituted a justiciable controversy in which no other court could afford speedy and adequate relief. The court assumed jurisdiction and ruled that a constitutional oath effected a repeal by substitution of the statutory oath. The court stated that no public official was required to take an oath in that form to qualify for office, and the governor did not forfeit his office. The court ruled that the senator had an interest in knowing that official interdepartmental affairs were being carried out legitimately, therefore he retained standing. However, that the 1969 amendment of the provisions of Okla. Const. art. XV, § 1 was intended to revamp the oath regime and repealed the previously enacted statutory law. There was no indicia of an intended transformation of the statutory provision into a mandatory, supplemental oath.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the constitutional oath effected a repeal by substitution of the statutory oath in the senator's action to disqualify the governor. The court ruled that no public official was required to take an oath in that form to qualify for office, and the governor did not forfeit his office.

 

79 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Rodebush v. Okla. Nursing Homes, Ltd.


No. 73,340


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 160; 867 P.2d 1241; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 188; 64 O.B.A.J. 3709



December 14, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE OKLAHOMA COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION II. Honorable Gordon R. Melson



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COA OPINION VACATED; JUDGMENT OF DISTRICT COURT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Certiorari was granted to the Oklahoma Court of Appeals, Division II after it affirmed a judgment against defendant nursing home and awarded plaintiff resident actual damages of $ 50,000 and $ 1.2 Million in punitive damages pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 23, § 9 (1991), in the resident's action for negligence and willful misconduct. The nursing home sought certiorari.


OVERVIEW: A nursing home aide slapped a resident while bathing him. The nursing home contended that it was not responsible for its employee's intentional acts, there was no clear and convincing evidence to lift the punitive damage cap, and that § 9 violated its constitutional right to due process. The court held that the nursing home was liable for the intentional act of the aide. The nursing home was in the business of taking care of Alzheimer patients, and the aide had not deviated from his assigned duties, and was carrying out an assigned task when the slapping occurred. The court held that the initial inquiry as to whether the conduct of the nursing home through its employee was wanton and reckless was supported by clear and convincing, albeit circumstantial, evidence. The cap was properly lifted for consideration by the jury. The court also held that § 9 did not violate due process because it did not give the jury unfettered discretion. The combination of statutory law, jury instructions, post-trial procedures and appellate review provided adequate safeguards against the jury's unbridled exercise of discretion in arriving at an award of punitive damages.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for the nursing home resident.

 

80 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Romero v. Workers' Comp. Court, CCB, INC.


No. 79,280


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 150; 863 P.2d 1251; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 175; 64 O.B.A.J. 3476



November 16, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION I



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED. OPINION OF THE COURT OF APPEALS VACATED. JUDGMENT OF THE WORKERS' COMPENSATION COURT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to a writ of certiorari, respondents, an employer, the Oklahoma Workers' Compensation Court (WC Court), and the State Insurance Fund, sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals, Division I (Oklahoma), which reversed the WC Court's decision that denied recovery of a claim by petitioner doctor.


OVERVIEW: An employee was treated by the doctor, allegedly for an on-the-job injury. Neither he nor his employer filed the appropriate forms for a workers' compensation claim, and no adjudication was made that the employee's injuries occurred on-the-job. The doctor filed a claim with the WC Court, which held that it had no jurisdiction because the appropriate forms had not been filed. The appellate court, upon reconsideration after vacating that order and remanding, held that the WC Court had exclusive jurisdiction pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 85, § 14(E) (1991). Upon further appeal, the court vacated the appellate court's decision. After reviewing § 14(E) in the context of the entire Title 85, the court held that an amendment to that section merely limited the jurisdiction of the WC Court when the appropriate forms had been filed. The court noted that Okla. Stat. tit. 85, § 3.4 (1991), provided that jurisdiction did not vest until a claim was filed pursuant to the Workers' Compensation Act. The court found that the doctor was not unconstitutionally deprived of a remedy as he could pursue an action against the employee directly for services rendered.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision of the appellate court and affirmed the decision of the WC Court.

Koch Fuels, Inc. v. Okla. ex rel. Okla. Tax Comm'n


No. 75,943


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 140; 862 P.2d 471; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 161; 64 O.B.A.J. 3276



October 26, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM ORDER OF THE OKLAHOMA TAX COMMISSION



DISPOSITION: ORDER 90-06-07-028 OF THE TAX COMMISSION REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant seller challenged an order of the Tax Commission (Oklahoma), which upheld the validity of the sales tax imposed under Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 1354 and made a tax assessment in favor of the State of Oklahoma.


OVERVIEW: A seller of fuel oil sought reversal of an order of the Tax Commission imposing an assessment for sales taxes, contending that a transfer of oil through an interstate pipeline was not a sale of tangible personal property subject to tax, and, if any sale occurred, the tax violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The court reversed the order and remanded the matter. Although the transaction constituted a sale as defined in Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 1352(L) because the f.o.b. delivery point of the oil was within the state, the exemption in Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 1359(E) for goods manufactured within the state for transport to another state was unconstitutionally discriminatory. In applying a four-prong test, the court considered: (1) whether the tax was applied to an activity with a substantial nexus with the taxing state; (2) whether the tax was fairly apportioned; (3) whether the tax discriminated against interstate commerce, and; (4) whether the tax was fairly related to the services provided by the state. Section 1359(E) discriminated against out-of-state manufacturers and violated the Commerce Clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Tax Commission and remanded the cause.

State ex rel. Okla. Bar Ass'n v. Gasaway


No. SCBD 3776


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 133; 863 P.2d 1189; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 158



October 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING FOR LAWYER DISCIPLINE. No. OBAD 1033



DISPOSITION: RESPONDENT DISBARRED AND COSTS IMPOSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter was before the court on the petition of complainant, the Oklahoma Bar Association, to disbar respondent attorney from the practice of law pursuant to Okla. R. Disc. P. 1.4(c), Okla. Stat. tit. 5, ch. 1, app. 1-A (1991).


OVERVIEW: The Association initially filed counts of professional misconduct against the attorney. After he failed to answer, the association prepared a proposed resignation from the bar. He declined to use that form and filed a "resignation in lieu of answer." The association objected to his resignation. Subsequently, the Professional Responsibility Tribunal recommended disbarment. The association then filed its petition to disbar him, which the court granted. The attorney's unsuccessful attempt to resign did not deprive the court of jurisdiction over the disciplinary proceedings. He was not entitled to a stay of the disciplinary proceedings because a criminal proceeding was pending against him. The attorney did not have a Fourth Amendment privacy interest with regard to the bank records because the subpoenas were not overbroad. It was erroneous for the Tribunal to deem the allegations admitted because the attorney's failure to answer was based on his assertion of his privilege against self-incrimination. However, based on the fact that the attorney had previously been suspended and privately reprimanded and based on his multiple acts of commingling clients' funds, disbarment was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court entered an order disbarring the attorney and imposed costs on him.

Walton v. Colonial Penn Ins. Co.


No. 78,339


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 115; 860 P.2d 222; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 140; 64 O.B.A.J. 2876



September 28, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ON CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION 3



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED, COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED, AND TRIAL COURT JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oklahoma) that found, in a case filed by appellee insured, that Okla. Stat. tit. 36, § 4803G was unconstitutional in that its one year statute of limitations for fire insurance claims conflicted with the general five year statute of limitations for contractual claims. Thus, the lower court found that it was a "special or local act" in violation of Okla. Const. art. 5, §§ 46, 59.


OVERVIEW: The insured filed suit to recover on a fire insurance policy held with the insurer. The trial court dismissed the suit without prejudice. The insured refiled more than one year from the date of the first dismissal, and that date of filing was without the one year statute of limitations set forth in Okla. Stat. tit. 36, § 4803G. The date of filing was also more than two years after the fire. The trial court dismissed the petition as time barred. The appellate court, however, found § 4803G unconstitutional. The court granted certiorari. On review, the court found that the insured's contention that the statute was a "special or local act" did not violate the Oklahoma Constitution because it differed from the general five year statute of limitations set forth at Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 95. The court found that insurance contract statutes of limitations could be set at different lengths by the legislature and that fire insurance contracts were no exception to that rule.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the appellate court and affirmed that of the trial court.

Strelecki v. Okla. Tax Comm'n

September 28, 1993, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ON APPEAL FROM THE OKLAHOMA TAX COMMISSION



DISPOSITION: THE TAX COMMISSION'S ORDER IS VACATED AND THE CAUSE REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant retirees petitioned for refunds of state income tax overpayments on their federal retirement income excludable under a new federal law. The retirees appealed the decision of appellee Oklahoma Tax Commission, which denied the refunds.


OVERVIEW: The issue presented was whether a new rule of federal law that excluded federal retirement income from state income tax applied retroactively to require refunds on the retirees' timely claims for a correct amount of state income tax overpayments. The court held that the new federal law applied retroactively to the retirees, and they were entitled to the refunds. The court held that it was stipulated below as to the correctness and timeliness of the refund claims until the denial of the claims. The court held that the Commission was bound by its earlier position. Accordingly, the court remanded the cause to the agency from which it came with directions to allow the timely refund claims together with any interest to which the retirees were entitled.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the action with directions to allow the timely refunds plus any interest calculated as due on the claims.

Further, the court determined that special appropriation bills were also required to contain a single subject. The court found that the proposed bills were not able to withstand a single-subject challenge. The court held that the bills were unconstitutional and granted the Representatives' motion for declaratory relief.

Muskogee Urban Renewal Auth. v. Excise Bd.


No. 74,424


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 Okla. LEXIS 102; 64 O.B.A.J. 1924



June 15, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] ON APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT IN MUSKOGEE COUNTY, THE HONORABLE BRUCE SEWELL, DISTRICT JUDGE



DISPOSITION: TRIAL COURT WRIT OF MANDAMUS IS DISSOLVED; TRIAL COURT JUDGMENT REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county, a county excise board, and a bank, challenged the judgment of the District Court in Muskogee County (Oklahoma), which held in favor of appellees, an urban renewal authority and a city, in an action to compel the apportionment of ad valorem tax revenues. The trial court issued a writ of mandamus and declared that Okla. Stat. tit. 11, §§ 38-120 et seq. (Supp. 1987) was not violative of Okla. Const. art. X, § 26.


OVERVIEW: The county excise board refused to apportion ad valorem tax revenues to the city for payment of a 30 year bond debt of the urban renewal authority. The board asserted that Okla. Stat. tit. 11, § 38-123 was unconstitutional. The court held that the statutory scheme for financing and paying the costs of urban renewal established in Okla. Stat. tit. 11, § 38-115 (Supp. 1983) and Okla. Stat. tit. 11, §§ 38-120, et seq., contravened Okla. Const. art. X, § 26. The inconsistent positions required of a municipality to finance urban renewal under the tax increment allocation statutes demonstrated an intent to frustrate the express terms, intent, and meaning of art. X, § 26. The 1983 statutory tax increment financing mechanism required apportionment of ad valorem tax revenues to a special fund for the payment of a long-term debt of an urban renewal authority, without requiring that the debt be certified as being within the municipality's debt limitations and be approved by a vote of the people. The mandated apportionment of ad valorem tax millage and revenue generated thereby in Okla. Stat. tit. 11, § 38-123 was in contravention of the intent and meaning of Okla. Const. art. X, § 26.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the urban renewal authority and city. The court dissolved the writ of mandamus that directed the county excise board to apportion ad valorem tax revenues.

SIMPSON v. DIXON


No. 81,318


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 71; 853 P.2d 176; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 89; 64 O.B.A.J. 1721



May 21, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING FOR A PREROGATIVE WRIT



DISPOSITION: ORIGINAL JURISDICTION ASSUMED; PETITION FOR WRITS OF PROHIBITION AND MANDAMUS DENIED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner alleged winner filed petition for writs of prohibition and mandamus, after respondent trial judge threw out the city council election results, to prohibit respondent county election board secretary from notifying the governor and to direct that a certificate of election be issued to her by the county election board because she defeated respondent opponent.


OVERVIEW: The nominee was initially determined as the successful candidate at the primary election for city council. Her opponent filed a protest. The nominee filed a motion to dismiss her opponent's petition as untimely. She argued that Oklahoma City, Okla. Charter art. 10, § 7 prescribed a two-day deadline for challenging the election results and that her opponent's petition, which was filed on the third day in conformity to state law, Okla. Stat. tit. 26, § 8-109 (1991), was too late. The motion was denied. The trial court found in favor of the opponent and directed the county election board to notify the governor. The nominee filed a petition for writs of prohibition and mandamus, which was denied, by the court. The court found that the opponent's failure explicitly to press for the applicability of Okla. Const. art. 3, § 5 and art. 5, § 46 was no impediment to its sua sponte invocation of the controlling constitutional commands. The court found that there was nothing in the text of the city charter from which it could infer that the drafters intended to confine an election protest to two days. The court noted that the State had a nondelegable duty to control the election-return process.


OUTCOME: The court assumed original jurisdiction. The court denied the nominee's petition for writs of prohibition and mandamus.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee worker filed a petition for a writ of certiorari to the Court of Appeals, Division 2 (Oklahoma), which reversed the decision of appellee, the Oklahoma Workers' Compensation Court, that the worker, who had received an out-of-state award for permanent disability, qualified as a previously impaired person under Okla. Stat. tit. 85, § 171 (1991).


OVERVIEW: The worker had been injured and recovered workers' compensation awards in Texas that included a determination of permanent partial disability. After he was injured in Oklahoma he filed an initial claim that was settled by the employee, the employer, and appellant insurer. The worker filed another claim against the insurer that alleged a material increase in disability as a result of the cumulative effect of the Oklahoma injury coupled with his two earlier injuries in Texas. The trial court found that the worker qualified as previously impaired, and the insurer appealed. The appellate court reversed the trial court, and the worker filed a petition for certiorari. The court determined that pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 85, § 171 (1991), a worker who had received an out-of-state workers' compensation award for permanent disability did not qualify as a previously impaired person, and that excluding out-of-state workers' compensation awards in the determination of an employee's status as a previously impaired person was not unconstitutional under the equal protection or full faith and credit tests. The State had an interest in protection of its statutory scheme.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision of the court of appeals and reversed the judgment of the trial court.

The court specifically determined that §§ 2-25, 27-32, 34, 35, 39, 41, 43, 44, and 45 were unconstitutional, §§ 1, 26, 33, 36, 40, and 46-49 were constitutional, and § 42 was constitutional in part.

Hooper v. Clinkingbeard 9In re Estate of Flowers)


No. 77,386


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 19; 848 P.2d 1146; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 31; 64 O.B.A.J. 813



March 16, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION III. Honorable Franklin D. Rahhal, Judge



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED; AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant beneficiaries challenged a decision from the Court of Appeals, Division III (Oklahoma), which affirmed a trial court order finding that appellee adopted daughter qualified as a pretermitted heir and that nothing on the face of the testatrix's will indicated an ambiguity allowing the introduction of extrinsic evidence to ascertain the adoptive mother's testamentary intent.


OVERVIEW: An order terminated parental rights of the adoptive mother. More than 45 years later, the adoptive mother executed a will naming her sisters beneficiaries. After her death, the adopted daughter filed a petition to receive the estate as a pretermitted heir pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 84, § 132 (1991). The beneficiaries objected, and the trial court entered an order in favor of the adopted daughter. The appeals court affirmed, but certiorari review, the court vacated that decision. The court held that the passage of the Uniform Adoption Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 10, § 60.1 et seq. (1991) abolished all pre-existing differences between adopted and natural children. Because the clear language of Okla. Stat. tit. 10, § 1132 (1991) provided that termination of parental rights did not affect a child's right to inherit from its parent, the adopted daughter qualified as a pretermitted heir under Okla. Stat. tit. 84, § 132 (1991). The existence of an order terminating the adoptive mother's parental rights was an extraneous fact rendering the will ambiguous, and parol evidence was admissible to ascertain the adoptive mother's intent.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appeals court's opinion, affirming in part, reversing in part, and remanding.

In re Termination of Parental Rights, Sara O.


NO. 75,604


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 10; 847 P.2d 768; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 15; 64 O.B.A.J. 576



February 16, 1993, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE OKLAHOMA COURT OF APPEALS, TEMPORARY DIVISION XXXXII



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; OPINION OF COURT OF APPEALS VACATED; ORDER OF DISTRICT COURT AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought certiorari review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oklahoma), which reversed the trial court's decision in favor of the mother in her action against appellee father for termination of his parental rights.


OVERVIEW: The father's parental rights were terminated for non-payment of child support by the trial judge. The court of appeals reversed and found that the father had been unconstitutionally deprived of a jury trial. On certiorari review, the court reinstated and affirmed the order of the trial court. The court noted that the father had attacked the trial court's decision to exclude his offer of evidence for the period in question when he paid no child support and that his ex-wife denied him visitation except under her supervision. The court noted that the entitlement to child support was not contingent upon visitation rights. The court stated that if the father felt aggrieved regarding visitation, he had access to the courts on that subject. The court held that the trial court was correct in sustaining the mother's objection to the father's complaints concerning visitation, as these complaints were irrelevant under Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 2401 (1981), which provided that child support was not dependent on child visitation. The court also noted that the father was employed full-time and had been for quite some time. The court affirmed the termination of the father's parental rights.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the court of appeals and reinstated and affirmed the judgment of the trial court that held that it was proper to terminate the father's parental rights.

ROSS v. PETERS


No. 76,059


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1993 OK 8; 846 P.2d 1107; 1993 Okla. LEXIS 12; 64 O.B.A.J. 440



February 9, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ON APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT, OKLAHOMA COUNTY. James L. Gullett, Judge



DISPOSITION: THE TRIAL COURT'S SUMMARY JUDGMENT IS AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant court reporters challenged a summary judgment from the District Court, Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), for appellee members of the State Board of Examiners of Official Shorthand Reporters (Board), in an action to construe Okla. Stat. tit. 20, § 1501 et seq. (1981). The court reporters urged that the Board could not refuse to enroll them as certified shorthand reporters under the statute, and that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court reporters used the stenomask method of reporting, and applied to the Board to take the examination for enrollment as certified shorthand reporters. One court reporter sought reciprocity. The Board denied the applications because the stenomask method was not one of the statutorily approved methods of showing proficiency in court reporting under Okla. Stat. tit. 20, § 1503 (1981). The court reporters brought an action to declare that Okla. Stat. tit. 20, § 1501 et seq. (1981) did not authorize the Board to refuse their certification, and if they did, then the statutes were unconstitutional. The trial court granted summary judgment to the Board, and the court reporters appealed. The court affirmed, explaining that Okla. Stat. tit. 20, § 1503(b), (c) required applicants for enrollment as certified shorthand reporters to take a test demonstrating proficiency in reporting using written symbols with "stenotype or similar machines." It concluded that "similar machines" did not include a stenomask, which relied on electronic recordation. Thus, the Board had had statutory authority to deny the court reporters' applications. The court found the statutes constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment.

Woods v. City of Lawton


No. 70,662


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 167; 845 P.2d 880; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 226; 64 O.B.A.J. 17



December 22, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION II. APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF COMANCHE COUNTY. Honorable Kenneth L. Youngblood, Trial Judge



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS' OPINION VACATED; JUDGMENT OF TRIAL COURT AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the City Board of Commissioners and Employee Retirement System of the City, sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals, Division II (Oklahoma), which reversed a judgment of the trial court in their favor. The trial court determined that Lawton, Okla. Ordinance § 17-108(f) was valid and constitutional as applied to appellee employee.


OVERVIEW: The employee contended that his right to his pension vested at the time he was eligible for retirement and could not be abrogated by Lawton, Okla. Ordinance § 17-108(f), which required forfeiture of all rights and benefits under the retirement system upon conviction for theft or embezzlement if the offenses were committed prior to retirement. The ordinance precluded vesting if the employee committed the specified offenses prior to his retirement, which in fact he did do. Any other interpretation would circumvent the intent of the ordinance and render it meaningless. The employee breached the contractual terms of the ordinance by committing offenses against the City while employed and thereby, forfeited his right to pension benefits. His contention that because he began receiving his pension in 1985, his criminal actions were somehow absolved so as to vest his right lacked merit. The employee's only right was to the return of his accumulated contributions acquired as of the date of his termination, as provided for by the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appeals court's judgment and affirmed the trial court's judgment against the employee.

Fowler v. Bailey


No. 67,146


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 160; 844 P.2d 141; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 223; 63 O.B.A.J. 3648



December 8, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] This Opinion Substituted for Withdrawn Opinion of September 25, 1990, Reported at 1990 Okla. LEXIS 98. As Corrected December 21, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: CERTIORARI TO THE OKLAHOMA COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION II



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED; JUDGMENT OF DISTRICT COURT AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff church members challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oklahoma), which summarily affirmed a judgment from a trial court dismissing the members claims against defendants, a church pastor, church deacons, and church trustees, after the members sought an injunction against excommunication, access to church financial records, and an accounting of church assets.


OVERVIEW: After the members brought their claims against the church officials, the church immediately expelled the members by a unanimous vote of the congregation at a hastily called business meeting following a prayer service. On appeal, the court vacated the opinion of the appellate court, but affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the members' claims. Specifically, the court rejected the members' argument that their expulsion was void and that they were entitled to a civil judicial order reinstating them as church members. Instead, the court held that the case was not a property dispute subject to civil authority because when the members were expelled, the members lost standing to prosecute the action for the benefit of the other church members. In addition, the court rejected the members' argument that they were the equivalent of stockholders in a business corporation filing a lawsuit to gain access to the financial records of a corporation and that they were entitled to inspect church records, pursuant to the Oklahoma Business Corporation Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 18, § 1.71 (1981) (repealed). Rather, the court held that § 1.71 did not apply to a nonprofit, non-stock church.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's judgment, which had summarily affirmed the trial court's judgment. As a result, the court affirmed the trial court's opinion dismissing the action.

 

95 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Jaworsky v. Frolich


No. 72,710


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 157; 850 P.2d 1052; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 213; 63 O.B.A.J. 3411; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P774



November 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ON CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS DIVISION 1



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED; TRIAL COURT'S ORDER AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the second owners of a residential home, filed an action for breach of implied warranty and negligence against appellee house builder. The owners appealed after the trial court found that the action was not timely filed and entered summary judgment for the builder. The builder sought further review after the Oklahoma Court of Appeals, Division 1, reversed and remanded the cause for trial.


OVERVIEW: The house was completed in 1976. Several years after they bought it, the owners noticed separations and cracks throughout the home. They filed an action in 1987 against the builder, asserting breach of the implied warranties of habitability and workmanlike construction and negligence. The trial court held that the action was subject to the 10-year limit imposed by Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 109 (1981) on tort actions arising out of an improvement to real property. The appeals court, however, held that the breach of warranty action was timely filed because the cause did not arise until the owners discovered the defective construction and they had filed the action within five years of that discovery. In reversing, the court held that the breach of warranty claims were contract-based claims not covered by § 109; they were covered by the five-year limitation imposed by Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 95 First (1981). The five-year limit began to run when the house was completed, not when defects were discovered. Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 109 (1981) was a statute of repose and imposed an absolute limit of 10 years for bringing tort actions arising out of the home's construction.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appeals court's opinion and affirmed the summary judgment entered by the trial court.

 

96 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Kerr v. Welker Elevator


No. 75,140


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 144; 839 P.2d 1360; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 205; 63 O.B.A.J. 3021



October 20, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected October 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION 2. APPEAL FROM THE WORKERS'S COMPENSATION COURT. HONORABLE BEN P. CHOATE, TRIAL JUDGE



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED. COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED. ORDER OF THE THREE-JUDGE PANEL SUSTAINED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent employer sought review of an opinion of the Court of Appeals (Oklahoma), which reversed the three-judge panel of the workers' compensation court and affirmed the trial court's award of temporary total disability and permanent partial disability benefits that were increased pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 85, § 21(5), because petitioner claimant was a minor.


OVERVIEW: The claimant, when he was 19 years old, was injured in the course of his employment, and his leg was amputated as a result. The workers' compensation court trial judge increased the claimant's award of temporary total disability and permanent partial disability benefits pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 85, § 21(5) on the basis that the claimant was a minor at the time of the injury. The three-judge panel of the workers' compensation court reduced the benefits awarded to the claimant on the basis that he was not a minor. The court of appeals reversed the three-judge panel and affirmed the trial court's award. The court vacated the opinion of the court of appeals and sustained the order of the three-judge panel, holding that minority status under § 21(5) ended when the claimant reached the age of 18 years because the workers' compensation act did not define the term "minor" and therefore Okla. Stat. tit. 15, § 13, applied, which established the age of majority at 18 years in any instance where it was not otherwise provided by law.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the opinion of the court of appeals and sustained the order of the three-judge panel of the workers' compensation court, which reduced the benefits awarded to the claimant on the basis that he was not a minor.

 

97 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


City of Chandler v. Okla. ex rel. Dep't of Human Servs.


No. 74,906


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 137; 839 P.2d 1352; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 193; 63 O.B.A.J. 2920



October 6, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF OKLAHOMA COUNTY, OKLAHOMA. HONORABLE JAMES B. BLEVINS, TRIAL JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE TRIAL COURT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the City of Chandler, challenged a decision of the District Court of Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which entered a declaratory judgment for defendant Department of Human Services (DHS) and defendant public trust that determined that Okla. Stat. tit. 56, § 189a (1981) authorized the DHS to relocate its Lincoln County office to the City of Stroud although the City of Chandler was the county seat of Lincoln County.


OVERVIEW: The DHS maintained local offices in each county in Oklahoma to conduct its state governmental operations with most of these offices located in the county seats pursuant to § 189a. The City of Chandler, the county seat of Lincoln County, provided some space to the DHS, but it was inadequate. The public trust offered to provide office space to the DHS in the City of Stroud. The DHS voted to accept the offer and relocate to Stroud, rejecting Chandler's proposal to provide additional space. Chandler filed suit, seeking a declaration that the DHS exceeded its authority under § 189a when it entered into a lease agreement with Stroud and further requesting an injunction to prohibit it from moving its office to Stroud. The trial court held that § 189a authorized the DHS to relocate its Lincoln County office to Stroud. On appeal, the court affirmed. The record showed that the DHS wholly complied with the statutory directives in seeking adequate and suitable office space. Adequate and suitable quarters were not available in the courthouse or other building in the county seat, and the DHS followed the plan established by the Legislature in locating a local office under those circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment.

 

98 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


In re Initiative Petition No. 349 State Question No. 642


No. 76,437


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 122; 838 P.2d 1; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 177; 65 O.B.A.J. 3225



August 4, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected August 5, 1992. As Corrected August 18, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING TO DETERMINE THE VALIDITY OF INITIATIVE PETITION NO. 349, STATE QUESTION NO. 642; AND APPEAL OF THE BALLOT TITLE.



DISPOSITION: INITIATIVE PETITION INVALID; ORDERED STRICKEN FROM THE BALLOT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The proponents filed an initiative petition to be on the ballot. The petition criminalized and absolutely prohibited abortions except in instances of grave impairment of the female's physical or mental health, rape, incest, and grave physical or mental defect of the fetus. The protestants filed a protest of the legal sufficiency of the petition. The Secretary of State filed the proposed ballot title, and the proponents filed a timely appeal.


OVERVIEW: Under the petition, if an abortion did not fall within one of the exceptions, any person who performed an abortion was punishable by up to four years imprisonment. Furthermore, the petition did not allow a woman to make a private decision to obtain an abortion at any time during the pregnancy, either before or after viability. On appeal, the court struck the petition from the ballot. The petition was unconstitutional because women who did not fall within the narrowly defined exceptions could not exercise the pre-viability liberty interest recognized by the United States Supreme Court. Because the Supreme Court had spoken, the court was not free to impose its own view of the law as it pertained to the competing interests involved. The existence of a severability provision was of no avail because without the inclusion of the petition's restrictions, the remainder of the proposed legislation was meaningless. The court further held that a presubmission determination of the constitutionality of the petition was appropriate and necessary because the proposal was facially unconstitutional and was justified when a costly and futile election could be avoided.


OUTCOME: The court declared the initiative petition invalid and struck such petition from the ballot.

 

99 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Riley v. Brown & Root, Inc.


No. 76,686


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 114; 836 P.2d 1298; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 147; 63 O.B.A.J. 2142; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P13,246



July 14, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected August 4, 1992. Second Correction August 24, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CERTIFIED QUESTION FROM THE UNITED STATES DISTRICT COURT FOR THE WESTERN DISTRICT OF OKLAHOMA. Honorable Lee R. West



DISPOSITION: QUESTION ANSWERED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the Western District of Oklahoma certified the following question to the court: Is Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 109 (1981) constitutional in wrongful death actions in light of Okla. Const. art. 23, § 7? Plaintiff personal representative filed a manufacture's products liability action against defendant manufacture and alleged that the deceased was killed due to a defectively designed base machine chest.


OVERVIEW: The deceased was killed when he entered the base machine chest to clean it. The trial court found that the chest was an improvement to real property within the definition of Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 109 (1981), which barred the representative's action when the death occurred more than 10 years after the completion of the improvement. The representative argued that § 109 violated Okla. Const. art. 23, § 107, which protected the right of action for wrongful death. The court found that Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 109 (1981) was constitutional. The court held that Okla. Const. art. 23, § 107 did not create a right of action; it protected the right of action as it existed when the constitution was adopted. According to the court, Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 1053 allowed the representative to maintain the wrongful death action only if the deceased might have maintained an action had he lived. The court held that the deceased would not have had a cause of action against the manufacturer had he lived. Thus, the court held that Okla. Stat. tit. 12, §109 did not reimpose death as a barrier to an action in violation of Okla. Const. art. 23, § 107.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified question by stating that the statute was constitutional.

Okla. ex rel. Okla. Bar Ass'n v. Smolen

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Complainant, the Oklahoma Bar Association, filed an action against respondent attorney, charging him with misconduct warranting professional discipline. The case was before the court for imposition of final discipline.


OVERVIEW: The attorney loaned money to numerous clients. The loans were non-interest bearing. The attorney loaned money for humanitarian reasons to clients who were destitute, without other means and resources or credit to obtain loans for sustenance during the pendency of their disability and in many cases could not work because of their injury. The parties entered proposed agreed stipulations of fact and conclusions of law with an agreed recommendation for discipline. The stipulations and recommendation were accepted and approved by the Trial Panel of the Professional Responsibility Tribunal (PRT). Thus, the court concluded that as discipline the attorney was to receive a public censure from the court.


OUTCOME: The court approved the proposed the parties' agreed stipulations of fact and conclusions of law with an agreed recommendation for discipline and ordered that the attorney was publicly censured.

Oklahoma Tax Com. v. City Vending of Muskogee, Inc.


No. 65,602


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 Okla. LEXIS 158; 63 O.B.A.J. 2287



July 14, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Granted July 14, 1992, Reported at 1992 Okla. LEXIS 157.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION I APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT OF MUSKOGEE COUNTY. Honorable William Bliss, Trial Judge



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED; TRIAL COURT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant vendor sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals, Division I (Oklahoma) that affirmed the trial court's granting of an injunction to appellee state tax commission preventing the vendor from selling cigarettes without a license.


OVERVIEW: The commission had adopted the administrative law judges recommendation and denied the vendor's protest to its assessment and cancelled the license for the distribution and sale of unstamped cigarettes. The vendor did not perfect an appeal with the court pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 225. The vendor contended that the order revoking his license was void ab initio, or facially void, for lack of jurisdiction. The court vacated the appellate court decision and affirmed the trial court order, finding that although the commission may not have been able to determine the constitutional defenses raised by the vendor before it, the court had the authority to consider those defenses on appeal and the vendor could not claim that the commission's order was void because it did not have authority to definitively determine the constitutional defenses. The court held that the Indian Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3, did not exempt all sales by a wholesaler to an Indian tribe. States could collect the tax from cigarette wholesalers, either by seizing unstamped cigarettes off the reservation or by assessing wholesalers who supplied unstamped cigarettes to the triable stores.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision of the court of appeals and affirmed the trial court's decision enjoining the vendor from selling from selling cigarettes without a license.

 

102 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Massey v. Farmers Ins. Group


No. 75,279


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 80; 837 P.2d 880; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 117



June 2, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 22, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer sought to overturn the judgment of a federal district court in favor of appellee insureds in their action to recover for a loss that was caused by fire. The United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit certified a question of law concerning the application of Okla. Stat. tit. 20, §§ 1601-1612 to the court.


OVERVIEW: The insurer invoked the mandatory appraisal provisions in Okla. Stat. Ann. tit. 36, § 4803 (1981) and refused to accept the results of an appraisal conducted by an umpire. They dismissed their state action without prejudice against the insurer to recover for a fire loss and filed an action in a federal district court. The insureds received a large verdict, which included actual, consequential, and punitive damages. The insurer appealed to the federal appellate court, which requested that the court answer the question as to whether an umpire's damage appraisal award had a preclusive effect. The court held that the umpire's appraisal award had no preclusive effect upon the insureds because they did not invoke the appraisal process. The court ruled that Okla. Const. art. 2, § 19 guaranteed the right to a jury trial in the insureds' action and that the insurer had no right to unilaterally terminate the insureds' jury trial rights where the mandatory appraisal process was required by state law. Even though the appraisal award was only binding with respect to the amount of damages suffered by the insureds, it still abridged their right to have all factual issues decided by a jury.


OUTCOME: The court answered the question posed by the federal appellate court and held that an appraisal award entered pursuant to state law had no preclusive effect upon the insureds in their action to recover for a loss that was caused by a fire.

So-Lo Oil Co. v. Total Petro., Inc.


No. 76,856


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 71; 832 P.2d 14; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 113; 1992-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P69,854



May 19, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 22, 1992. As Corrected June 1, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: CERTIFIED QUESTION FROM A UNITED STATES COURT



DISPOSITION: CERTIFIED QUESTION ANSWERED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The certified question was whether the provisions of the Oklahoma Unfair Sales Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 15, §§ 598.1-598.11, especially § 598.3, applied to entities such as defendant corporation which not only produced and refined petroleum products, but also sold those products at retail directly to the general public in competition with companies such as plaintiff distributor.


OVERVIEW: In a case of first impression, the court found that the Oklahoma Unfair Sales Act (Act), Okla. Stat. tit. 15, §§ 598.1-598.11 did not apply to entities such as defendant, which produced and refined petroleum products and also sold those products at retail directly to the general public in competition with distributors. The court reasoned that the Act was clearly anticompetitive and restricted the freedom to trade. The statute was strictly construed. The Act's cost definitions were directed exclusively to those associated with distribution. Had the legislature intended to include manufacturers, it would have defined production costs and cost accounting methods and provided the guidelines necessary to the Act's enforcement against manufacturers. The structure of the statute plainly showed an intent that the Act's ambit be confined to sales specifically defined in it.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified question no.

Merrill v. Okla. Tax Comm'n


No. 70,649


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 53; 831 P.2d 634; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 72; 63 O.B.A.J. 1291



April 28, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT, OKLAHOMA COUNTY, OKLAHOMA. David M. Cook, Judge



DISPOSITION: THE TRIAL COURT'S JUDGMENT IS AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney filed suit against defendant Oklahoma Tax Commission and claimed that he was entitled to copies of all records generated by the Commission in the discharge of its duties under the Uniform Disposition of Unclaimed Property Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 60, § 651 et seq. The District Court, Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), entered judgment against the attorney. The attorney appealed.


OVERVIEW: The attorney claimed that the Oklahoma Open Records Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 51, § 24A.1 et seq., entitled him to copies of all records generated by the Commission in the discharge of its duties under the Uniform Disposition of Unclaimed Property Act (Act), Okla. Stat. tit. 60, § 651 et seq., rather than only those the Commission considered public. He also claimed that the Commission's response to his request for public records was too slow and its copy fees excessive. He sought relief with attorney fees. The trial court held that all unclaimed property records were confidential, except those the Act required to be published, that the Commission's response time and proposed copy fees were reasonable, and that the fee request was legally unsupportable. On appeal, the court affirmed. In reaching its decision, the court found that the Commission satisfactorily met its burden of showing that all unclaimed property records not statutorily required to be made public were exempt from the purview of the Oklahoma Open Records Act. The court further held that the finding regarding the Commission's response time and copy fees was not against the clear weight of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals, Division 2 (Oklahoma), which reversed the order of appellee Oklahoma Corporation Commission, held that the Unclaimed Pooled Monies Act (UPMA) was unconstitutional as applied to the corporation, and that the UPMA would have been enforceable in three situations. OVERVIEW: The corporation applied for an exception to the UPMA, which would have allowed it to discontinue remittance of unclaimed funds for the 1986 reporting year. The court of appeals reversed the Commission order, holding that the UPMA was unconstitutional as applied to the corporation, although the UPMA would have been enforceable in three other situations. The court vacated the court of appeals' opinion, reinstated the Commission's order, and remanded the cause. Lincoln Bank & Trust Co. v. Okla. Tax Comm'n

SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE DISTRICT COURT, OKLAHOMA COUNTY, OKLAHOMA. Charles L. Owens, Judge



DISPOSITION: THE TRIAL COURT'S PERMANENT INJUNCTION DECREE IS REVERSED; CASE IS REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS TO DISSOLVE THE INJUNCTION AND TO ENFORCE THE COMMISSION'S ADMINISTRATIVE PROCESS IN ACCORDANCE WITH THIS PRONOUNCEMENT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Oklahoma Tax Commission challenged the judgment of the District Court, Oklahoma County (Oklahoma), which granted the request of appellees, a bank and bankers association, for an injunction to enjoin the Commission's administrative process for inspection of financial records.


OVERVIEW: The Commission notified the bank of its intent to inspect certain financial records and documents kept by the bank. The Commission said it had reason to believe the bank was holding unreported property presumed abandoned by the Uniform Disposition of Unclaimed Property Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 60, § 651 et seq. (1981). The bank and the association brought suit to quash, by injunctive relief, the Commission's administrative process for inspection of financial records. The court determined that the district court had jurisdiction to inquire into the underlying basis for administrative process by which the Commission sought to inspect the bank's financial records upon the belief that the bank had failed to report property deemed abandoned by the Act. The court found that the Financial Privacy Act, Okla. Stat. tit. 6, § 2201 et seq., did not pose a barrier to the Commission's search for unreported abandoned property via its own administrative process. The court further held that the Commission had a legally sufficient reason to believe that the bank had failed to comply with the reporting requirements of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of an injunction and remanded the case to the trial court with directions to dissolve the injunction and to enforce the Commission's administrative process.

Davis v. B. F. Goodrich


No. 72,080


SUPREME COURT OF OKLAHOMA


1992 OK 14; 826 P.2d 587; 1992 Okla. LEXIS 13; 63 O.B.A.J. 369



February 4, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As corrected February 12, 1992


PRIOR HISTORY: CERTIORARI TO THE COURT OF APPEALS, DIVISION III



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; COURT OF APPEALS' OPINION VACATED; ORDER OF WORKERS' COMPENSATION COURT SUSTAINED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner workers' compensation claimant alleged injury to his lungs and upper respiratory system caused by continuous exposure to hazardous chemicals in the workplace. The Workers' Compensation Court found that the claimant had not sustained or suffered any accidental injury arising out of and in the course of his employment. The Court of Appeals (Oklahoma) sustained the trial tribunal. The claimant petitioned for writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The claimant filed a workers' compensation claim alleging injury to his lungs and upper respiratory system caused by continuous exposure to hazardous chemicals, including industrial talc, and fumes, in the workplace. The claimant testified that he smoked "a pack of cigarettes a day and had been smoking for about 15 years at that rate." The claimant argued that the carbon monoxide diffusion test (D[co] test) was required because his complaints were of greater severity than the Spirometry test indicated. The respondent Workers' Compensation Court's doctor found that the history of dyspnea was consistent with the Spirometry tests. In sustaining, the court held that whether the complaints were of greater severity than the Spirometry results were within the "area of medical discretion that a court should not normally delve into." Thus, a D[co] test was not always required before a claimant could be rated at zero impairment.


OUTCOME: Certiorari was granted. The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated, and the order of the Workers' Compensation Court was sustained.

Bd. of County Comm'rs v. City of Muskogee


No. 72,761 consolidated with No. 72,748


Supreme Court of Oklahoma


1991 OK 115; 820 P.2d 797; 1991 Okla. LEXIS 124; 62 O.B.A.J. 3429



November 5, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the District Court, Muskogee County, G. Bruce Sewell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: THE TRIAL COURT'S ORDER IN MANDAMUS IS AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART AND CAUSE REMANDED FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH TODAY'S PRONOUNCEMENT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county commissioners appealed a judgment of the District Court, Muskogee County (Oklahoma), which refused to issue a writ of mandamus against defendant city to compel payment of revaluation costs incurred in determining ad valorem tax revenues. Defendant school board appealed the trial court's issuance of a writ of mandamus to the county commissioners to compel its payment of revaluation costs.


OVERVIEW: The commissioners revaluated all county property in compliance with Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 2481.1 et seq. (1984). The commissioners billed the city and the school board for their proportionate share of the revaluation costs. The city and school board refused to pay these costs. The trial court issued a writ of mandamus to the commissioners that compelled the school board to pay the costs, but denied the writ against the city. The court affirmed the granting of the writ against the school board but reversed the denial of the writ against the city. The court held that the program of revaluation of taxable county property imposed a duty on the city and school board to pay their share of the revaluation costs. The city was a statutory beneficiary of ad valorem levies. The city's lack of control over the tax assessment process did not make its cost liability constitutionally impermissible. The statutory scheme of assessment did not violate the constitution. The ad valorem millage rates allocated to the school district were strictly for educational purposes. The court viewed the arrangement for sharing the cost of revaluing property as the school board's quid pro quo.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order in mandamus that granted the writ against the school board. The court reversed that part of the order that denied the writ against the city and remanded the cause for further proceedings.

In re Initiative Petition No. 348, State Question No. 640


No. 76,277


Supreme Court of Oklahoma


1991 OK 110; 820 P.2d 772; 1991 Okla. LEXIS 122; 62 O.B.A.J. 3387



October 29, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected, February 21, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Proceeding to Determine the Validity of Initiative Petition No. 348, State Question No. 640.



DISPOSITION: INITIATIVE PETITION NO. 348 IS DECLARED LEGALLY SUFFICIENT FOR SUBMISSION TO THE PEOPLE OF OKLAHOMA.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This was an original action brought by protestants pursuant to Okla. Stat. tit. 34, § 8 (1981) to challenge the legality of Initiative Petition 348, State Question 640 (Petition). The Petition purported to amend by the initiative process Okla. Const. art. V, § 33. The amendment imposed severe limitations upon the legislature's ability to raise new revenue.


OVERVIEW: The Petition required all revenue raising bills to be approved by a majority of the voters unless the revenue bill was approved by a three-fourths vote of both houses. The protestants asserted the Petition violated Okla. Const. art. XXIV, § 1 because it embraced more than one general subject; that the Petition was misleading and deceptive and violative of Okla. Stat. tit. 34, §§ 3, 9 (1981); that it conflicted with other articles of the constitution; and threatened the finance of state government. The protestants further argued the Petition contravened the guarantee of a republican form of government. The court held that the only subject matter addressed in the petition was a procedural requirement allowing for direct participation by the people in the enactment of revenue measures. The protestants' argument about destruction of state financing was without merit as the specific legislative grants of power were subject to the reserved power of the people under Okla. Const. art. IV § 1. The gist and ballot title appraised the voters what the measure was intended to do. The Petition did not destroy the representative form of government, just by an impact on the governmental framework.


OUTCOME: The Petition was legally sufficient for submission to the people of Oklahoma and the ballot title presented by the Attorney General was approved.

 

110 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Walters


No. 77,919


Supreme Court of Oklahoma


1991 OK 107; 819 P.2d 694; 1991 Okla. LEXIS 123; 62 O.B.A.J. 3397



October 29, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Application to Assume Original Jurisdiction and Petition for Writ of Porhibition.



DISPOSITION: APPLICATION TO ASSUME ORIGINAL JURISDICTION GRANTED; PETITION FOR WRIT OF PROHIBITION GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the Speaker of the Oklahoma House of Representatives and the President Pro Tempore of the Oklahoma Senate, filed an application for the court to assume original jurisdiction and sought petition for a writ of prohibition to be issued to respondents, agencies, boards, commissions, and officers, to prevent implementation of all general legislation provisions of two bills partially approved an partially vetoed by respondent Governor.


OVERVIEW: The legislature sent two bills to the Governor for signature. The first bill contained more than one general legislation provision in violation of Okla. Const. art. V, § 57, which required one subject per bill, and the Governor treated them as separate bills for the purposes of approving or vetoing them. The second bill contained general legislation provisions and appropriations provisions. Okla. Const. art. VI, § 12 gave the Governor a restricted veto power with regard to appropriations only. He was not vested with a line-item veto power in any other context. He approved some of the appropriations. On petition for a writ of prohibition, the court assumed jurisdiction. It held that the leading case, State ex rel. Wiseman v. Oklahoma Board of Corrections, 614 P.2d 551 (Okla. 1978) did not adequately address the situation of the Governor's veto power in connection with the legislature's drafting of impermissible bills. It prospectively overruled the case. In the future the Governor was to treat a bill as unconstitutional in the event it had multiple provisions. He could not exercise a pocket veto power or treat the multiple provisions as separate bills for his consideration.


OUTCOME: The court granted the application to assume original jurisdiction, and the petition for a writ of prohibition was granted. Respondents were prohibited from implementing any portion of one bill and from implementing any portion of the second bill except for the items of appropriation contained in the bill and not disapproved by the Governor.

 

111 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Fed. Land Bank of Wichita v. Am. Bank & Trust Co.


No. 70,936


Supreme Court of Oklahoma


1991 OK 91; 823 P.2d 359; 1991 Okla. LEXIS 119; 62 O.B.A.J. 3381; 62 O.B.A.J. 3545



October 22, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 19, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Appeals, Division I; Appeal from the District Court of Rogers County, Oklahoma; Honorable Steven J. Adams, Trial Judge.



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED. COURT OF APPEALS OPINION VACATED. JUDGMENT OF THE DISTRICT COURT IS REVERSED AND REMANDED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a first mortgage holder on real property, disputed a decision of the Court of Appeals, Division I (Oklahoma), which affirmed a grant of summary judgment in favor of appellees, a holder of a second mortgage on the property and an individual who bought the property at a tax resale and received a resale tax deed. The issue was whether the notice of the tax resale appellant received complied with Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 24331 (Supp. 1984).


OVERVIEW: The document appellant received was entitled "Delinquent Tax Statement" and the bulk of the information listed in it concerned the delinquent taxes. The document contained no other language in the caption or the notice to suggest that it was notice of a tax resale of the property. The day after the tax resale, appellant filed a petition to foreclose its mortgage lien, which led to the action at bar. The trial court granted summary judgment on the theory that appellant's lien had been extinguished by the tax resale deed. On appeal, the court noted that the specific statutes that authorized the actions of a county treasurer in conducting a tax sale and resale were Okla. Stat. tit. 68, §§ 24311 to 24337 (1981). The court pointed out that, in particular, Okla. Stat. tit. 68, § 24331 (1981) governed the requirements as to the contents of a notice of tax resale sent to the owners of the subject property and to any mortgagees of record and the publication of such a notice in an appropriate newspaper. The court held that the notice appellant received was not sufficient to satisfy the notice requirements of § 24331. Thus, appellant's mortgage was not extinguished by the resale tax deed.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the opinion of the Court of Appeals, reversed the trial court judgment, and remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with the decision.

Ross v. Kelsey Hayes, Inc.


No. 77,018


Supreme Court of Oklahoma


1991 OK 83; 825 P.2d 1273; 1991 Okla. LEXIS 90; 62 O.B.A.J. 2428



July 30, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to Review a Certified Interlocutory Order; District of Oklahoma County; The Honorable Carolyn Ricks, Trial Judge. 



DISPOSITION: CERTIORARI PREVIOUSLY GRANTED; CAUSE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner manufacturers were granted their petition for certiorari to review a certified interlocutory order by the District of Oklahoma County court (Oklahoma), which denied their motion to dismiss a products liability action. Respondent filed an action within the limitations period and then prior to service of process voluntary dismissed the action and then refiled an action after the limitations period under Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 100 (1981).


OVERVIEW: Respondent's original action was filed within the two-year statute of imitations under Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 95 (1981). The new action was filed after the two-year period reflecting the previous case and its dismissal without prejudice. The manufacturers contended that the action was time barred because they were not notified of the original action by service of summons and that the action was not properly commenced under Okla. Stat. tit. 12, § 100 (1981). The manufacturers also contended that the savings provision of § 100 might not have been invoked to extend the statute of limitations for one year from the date of a voluntary dismissal if a plaintiff did not serve a defendant in the original action. The court held that an action was commenced within the meaning of § 100 by filing a petition and that service of process was not constitutionally required. The court held the action was commenced under § 100 by filing a petition and that because of the remedial nature of § 100's provisions they were to be liberally construed. Therefore, because the action was not barred, no constitutionally protectable interest in the statute of limitations arose, and the cause was remanded.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the cause.

 

113 of 1460 DOCUMENTS


Kay Elec. Coop. v. State ex rel. Okla. Tax Comm'n


No. 74,076 (Consolidated with No. 74,198 and No. 73,942)


Supreme Court of Oklahoma


1991 OK 76; 815 P.2d 175; 1991 Okla. LEXIS 85; 62 O.B.A.J. 2233



July 9, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Oklahoma Tax Commission.



DISPOSITION: ORDER OF TAX COMMISSION REVERSED. CAUSE REMANDED WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REFUND PROTESTED STATE SALES TAXES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee Oklahoma Tax Commission (the Commissioner) adopted Regulation 13-62 requiring rural electric cooperatives to collect, report, and remit state, city, and county taxes on the sale of electricity. Appellant cooperatives paid the sales tax under protest and filed for refunds. The Commission denied the cooperatives' requests for refunds. The cooperatives appealed.


OVERVIEW: In a prior decision, the court had addressed whether rural electric cooperatives were exempt from collecting, reporting, and remitting sales tax but had not addressed whether the tax should have been refunded. The Commission argued that the decision should only have been given prospective effect. The court reversed and held that, having found that the taxes were illegally collected, the Commission must refund the taxes. The general rule was that a decision was given retrospective application. The court acknowledged that there was an exception to that rule. However, there was no new pronouncement in the decision overruling clear past precedent on which the parties relied. There was also little inequity in imposing the decision retroactively. The Commission was aware of the possibility that the taxes would have to be refunded. While the Commission argued that it would endure hardship if the decision was applied retrospectively, it did not argue that it was unfair. Balancing these three factors, the court concluded that the scales tilted on the side of the general rule.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Commission's order and remanded with instructions to refund the protested state sales taxes to the cooperatives.
